==PAGE:9==
Abstract
The developments in solutions for wastewater management are of vital importance, as the amount of sewage
water from urban areas continues to increase due to the exponential increase of the worldβÄôs population,
and can have catastrophic effects on worldβÄôs biosphere. How a sewer network is structured, monitored and
controlled have thus become essential factors for efο§¹cient and effective performance of wastewater treatment
plants.
This thesis examines simpliο§¹cation based methods for modelling and controlling a sewer network with
the purpose of investigating factors inο§²uencing computational effort. A practical approach to a problem is
made by analyzing a designed model, based on the Barcelona benchmark model through the optimal control
method, Model Predictive Control, which is used for assessing the analysis of the normal and scalar input
and soft output constrained approaches.

Acronyms
LQR
Linear Quadratic Regulator.
MPC
Model Predictive Control.
QP
Quadratic Programming.
3TC
Three Tank Catchment model.
UC
Unconstrained MPC.
IC
Input Constrained MPC approach.
IIRC
Input and Input Rate Constrained MPC approach.
OC
Output Constrained MPC approach.
IOC
Input, Input rate and Output Constrained MPC approach.
ISOC
Input, Input rate and Soft Output Constrained MPC approach.
9TC
Nine Tank Catchment model.
WWTP
Wastewater Treatment Plant.
VT
Virtual Tank model.
HLD
Hybrid Linear Delayed model.
ID
Integrator Delay model.
IDZ
Integrator Delay model with Zero.
MIMO
Multiple Inputs Multiple Outputs.
LTI
Linear Time Invariant.
LTV
Linear Time Varying.

Nomenclature
V
Sequence of tank volume quantity [m3].
u
Sequence of control input signals i.e. inο§²ows to certain tanks [m3/s].
Έ≤
Volume/ο§²ow coefο§¹cient [sβà£1].
Ts
Sampling time [s].
A, B, Ex, C
Discrete time state space model matrices of the 3TC system.
At
Discrete time varying state matrix of the 3TC system.
I
Identity matrix.
Qw, Rw, P
Weighting matrices.
x
Sequence of states i.e. volume levels for system tanks.
d
Sequence of disturbance signals i.e. external inο§²ows to virtual tanks.
J, œÜ
Objective or cost function.
Mx0, MR, MD, Muβà£1
MPC gain matrices i.e. for system states, reference, disturbance and input rates,
respectively.
ΈΠ, Έ™u, Έ™d
Dynamical model matrices of certain prediction horizon.
Qz, Hs
Weighting matrices for MPC gain.
Ae, Be, Ex,e, Ce
Discrete time state space model matrices of the 9TC system.
Ae,t
Discrete time varying state matrix of the 9TC system.
N
Simulation sample horizon.
Nc
Control horizon.
Np
Prediction horizon.
min
Minimum value.
max
Maximum value.
n, nmin
Lower bound where n is a deο§¹ned variable.
n, nmax
Upper bound where n is a deο§¹ned variable.
k
Time step index.
tQP
The computation time it takes the QP solver to obtain a solution for each step of
simulation.
tCPU
The time it takes the computer to run the main simulation loop.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
Controlling sewer networks is a demanding and necessary task in modern society, that prevents property
damages, accidents and more importantly reduces the risk of environmental pollution. Therefore, it is
important to manage the sewage water to wastewater treatment plants (WWTPs) as efο§¹ciently as possible.
Inadequate or no treatment of sewage water1 can have a major impact on biodiversity in rivers, lakes and
the sea, where harmful bacteria and toxic chemicals that are carried by the sewage water, can cause serious
health problems and even lead to more deadly epidemic outbreaks such as malaria.
Many cities have combined sewer systems and the need for efο§¹cient control of sewer networks is going
to increase, especially according to some predictions stating that by the year 2050, 80% of the worldβÄôs
population will be living in urban areas[1].
1.2
Background
Sewer networks are considered complex systems, as they are geographically distributed and structured for
the collection of sewage and run-off 2 water. Usually, sewer systems are divided into two categories: separate
or combined. The separate sewer system uses different pipelines for sewage and run-off water, while in the
combined sewer system, sewage and run-off water are mixed together in the same pipeline. Whereas, the
separate sewer system directs sewage water to a WWTP, the run-off water is released into the environment,
treated or untreated, then the combined sewer system, sewage and run-off water are both transported to a
WWTP. As sewage water is considered to be more contaminated than run-off water, consequences become
more severe when an overο§²ow situation occurs in the combined sewer system, as the risk of contaminated
water escaping into the environment is higher.
Rainstorms are considered the main inο§²uencing factor that cause combined sewer systems to overο§²ow.
Therefore, minimizing overο§²ow situations in combined sewer system is a more demanding procedure than
for the separate system, making the overall control problem more difο§¹cult to manage[2].
In general, wastewater is categorized as originating from households, industry and sewage, while run-off
water arise when rainfalls occurs.
The main purpose of WWTPs, is to purify as much contaminated water as possible, before releasing it back
into the environment. Thus, the number of treatment plants needed for a speciο§¹c sewer network is dependent
on the quantity of wastewater in the plantsβÄô districts and their maximum inο§²ow capacity.
1In this thesis there will not be distinguished between sewage and waste water.
2Run-off water is formed when it is raining and ο§²ows off from earthβÄôs surface in streams.
1
==PAGE:28==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
This issue is most commonly addressed by controlling ο§²ow rates of sewer pipes to maximize treatment plant
performance. Furthermore, pipelines are limited by their dimensions and therefore ο§²ow quantities need to be
controlled within boundary limits of the pipes, by manipulating the ο§²ow rates in the pipes.
1.3
Thesis Objectives and Scope
The thesis objectives are to model and control a sewer network, while keeping the computational effort
low, with the higher purpose of minimizing the risk of overο§²ow of polluted water into the environment.
Consequently, as large scale sewer networks are considered complex systems, with their operating points
often close to their boundary limits due to external inο§²ows (rainfalls), the computation time for the optimal
control becomes problematic.
In this context the objectives of this thesis are:
1. Research modelling methods for sewer networks based on simpliο§¹cations.
2. Modelling of a sewer network system.
3. Analysis of sewer system components, boundaries and behavior.
4. Decide on applicable control strategies for the system.
5. Formulate and implement the control problem.
6. Analysis of the time factor and simpliο§¹cation procedures in order to minimize computational effort.
The foundation of the linear model used in this thesis will be built on the widely studied Barcelona sewer
network model and previous work carried out by Carlos A. Ocampo MartΟ≠nez [3], [4]. Since a nonlinear
dynamics of a physical model of a sewer network is not provided for this thesis. For the linear model, it is
assumed that the system linearized dynamics are valid for all operating points.
In addition, the linear model provided by MartΟ≠nez, will facilitate the construction of a mathematical bench-
mark model suitable for advanced control methods such as Model Predictive Control (MPC).
The main reason for choosing the MPC method as the control strategy for the sewer network, relies on its
unique ability to include boundary constraints in the optimization procedures for MIMO systems.
The control objectives for system performance are:
βÄΔ Minimize ο§²ooding to streets.
βÄΔ Maximize the wastewater treatment plant/plants (less pollution to the environment)
Notice that several issues are addressed by the two control objectives listed above. Firstly, by minimizing
ο§²ooding to streets, environmental hazards are prevented, such as property damages, accidents and polluting
the environment. Secondly, in order to maximize the treated wastewater, the distribution of ο§²ows within the
system must be controlled, so that the maximum capacity/capacities of WWTP/WWTPs is/are not exceeded
and the untreated wastewater received by the sea, minimized.
==PAGE:29==
1.4. OUTLINE OF THESIS
3
1.4
Outline of Thesis
This section describes the contents of each chapter of this thesis.
Chapter 2: Modelling Methods
In this chapter, the most dominant approach when modelling water characteristics in open channels is
introduced. Then, the most frequently used simpliο§¹ed modelling techniques for river and sewer network
control are presented and their properties evaluated and compared. The selected model is then further
described in Chapter 4.
Chapter 3: Control Formulation and Analysis
In this chapter, the optimization problems that need to be solved and the associated control procedures that
maximizes performance of the optimal controllers are introduced. The two widely used control design
methods, LQR and MPC, used for optimal control are then presented, with main focus on the MPC approach.
General descriptions are provided for various MPC methods i.e. their background information and properties
are discussed. Then, several MPC techniques are formulated and introduced based on complexity, starting
with the most simplest one.
Chapter 4: Case Study Model
The model selected in Chapter 2 is introduced as the case study model, and a description of the system is
provided, along with a block diagram of the model, in ο§¹gure 4.4a. The discrete linear mathematical model
describing the tanks geometric behavior, ο§²ow rates for associated pipes with gates and the relationship
between the tank volume and ο§²ow, is then presented. In addition, the linear state space description is obtained
using the model equations, which are further used for the controller design and analysis of the closed-loop
system. Finally, the relation between the linear and nonlinear system is analyzed.
Chapter 5: Implementation and Evaluation
This chapter describes different implementations that further concern aspects such as system performance,
structural constraints, controller design and procedures based on simpliο§¹cation. Firstly, the structural
constraints are evaluated as they are necessary for closed-loop simulation. Moreover, for the controller
design, two optimal control methods are compared. Further, for the system performance and analysis of the
closed-loop description, a tuning approach is formulated for the system weighting matrices. Then, a fast
MPC approach, referred to as the scalar ISOC, is formulated and explained, with simpliο§¹cations performed
on the normal ISOC MPC method.
Chapter 6: Three Tank Catchment Model Results
Analysis of the system performance and elapsed time of simulations, tCPU, is performed in this chapter on
the 3TC models closed-loop description. The speciο§¹cations for the analysis, i.e. motives and conο§¹guration
data for simulations, are presented and explained, and the corresponding results are discussed. The analyses
are then extended to better illustrate the system behavior as a necessary knowledge used in augmenting the
3TC model to a larger sewer network, in Chapter 7.
==PAGE:30==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Chapter 7: Augmenting the Sewer Network Model
Using the same methodology as for the modelling method applied on the 3TC model in Chapter 4, a larger
sewer network system is modelled and presented. The augmented model consists of a real tank, eight virtual
tanks, two redirection gates, a retention gate, three weir overο§²ow devices and two wastewater treatment
plants. The augmented model consists of nine tanks in total and will be referred to as the 9TC model. In
addition, the recursion of the discrete mathematical model equations and the state space model representation
is presented. System parametersβÄô numerical values are provided as well.
Chapter 8: Results of the Augmented Model
In this chapter, the 9TC model is analyzed through several closed-loop simulations on the designed model.
Further, same analysis performed for the 3TC system in Section 6.2 are carried out for the 9TC model.
Results regarding comparison of elapsed time between the normal and scalar MPC methods, for both systems
(3TC and 9TC) are compared along with the performance of the system.
Chapter 9: Conclusion and Future Work
In this chapter, the main results of the thesis are evaluated and compared to objectives in Section 1.3.
Furthermore, ideas for future work are proposed as suggestions for ongoing study.
==PAGE:31==
CHAPTER 2
Modelling Methods
2.1
Introduction
Modelling methods are analyzed in this chapter, in order to ο§¹nd a simple sewer network approach that is
applicable to the thesis objectives.
The literature and research material presented in this chapter is mostly based on Marinaki & Papageorgiou
[5], MartΟ≠nez [3], [4] and i Duran [6]. A suitable modelling method will be selected, which will be used for
ongoing analysis.
2.1.1
The Saint Venant Equations
The most general approach when modelling a sewer network, is to use the Saint Venant nonlinear partial
differential equations. Assuming that the movement of water is one dimensional, the water ο§²ow can be repre-
sented as an open channel where the distribution of pressure is hydrostatic3, the sewer pipesβÄô cross-sectional
areas are constant with a uniform velocity over the cross-section, the ο§²ow is incompressible and the average
slope in the channel is small4 [8].
The general Saint Venant equations are given as:

βÄΔ A(x, t) is the cross-sectional area of the pipe in m2.
βÄΔ t is the time variable in s.
βÄΔ x is the spatial variable measured in the direction of the sewage ο§²ow in m.
βÄΔ g is the gravitational acceleration in m/s2.
βÄΔ I0(x) is the sewer pipe slope (dimensionless).
βÄΔ If(x, t) is the friction slope (dimensionless).
In addition, a complete model of a sewer network is achieved based on the assumptions mentioned above, in
which the Saint Venant equations are applied for each sewer pipe in the sewer network. Each pipe in the
system will therefore have different geometrical structure and behavior. This further results in difο§¹culties
when obtaining an arbitrary solution for each sewer pipe, using eqs. 2.1 and 2.2. Thus, not a feasible
approach for real time control implementations, due to the high computation effort and level of details in the
Saint Venant equations [3], [6].
2.2
Modelling Types
Modelling methods for simpliο§¹ed models of sewer networks/channels that include the theoretical basis of
the Saint Venant equations are introduced and examined in this section. As the simplicity imposed by these
methods brings advantage when dealing with real time optimization problems for large scale sewer networks.
Furthermore, the methods introduced in this chapter have a common or similar approach when constructing
a simpliο§¹ed hydraulic model i.e. where the mass balance conservation formula is used and simpliο§¹ed, as
illustrated in eq. 2.5. The mathematical procedures when obtaining this relation and conversion factors is
then what differs between methods.

==PAGE:33==
2.2. MODELLING TYPES
7
The selected model must accommodate the assumptions mentioned in Section 2.1.1 along with the require-
ments of being applicable for advanced control strategy and requiring low computational effort compared to
the real system.
2.2.1
The Virtual Tank Model
The virtual tank (VT) model approach is widely used for real time control applications such as combined
sewer overο§²ows, wastewater treatment plant optimizations, irrigation channels and rivers. A detailed descrip-
tion of the VT model framework is presented in [5]. Several modelling techniques are introduced, along
with a simpliο§¹ed model based on an accurate model of a combined sewer network. In addition, techniques
to make the simple model more accurate an integrated simpliο§¹ed model is introduced as well. The Saint
Venant equations are used for the mathematical modelling of the accurate model. Thus, fundamental laws of
hydraulics are used to model various types of elements by using time delay or hydrodynamic link elements.
The time delay links are commonly placed in the system and used to model a uniform ο§²ow, where back
pressure effects are insigniο§¹cant or can be ignored. The hydrodynamic link elements replicate a more
accurate ο§²ow behavior and most frequently used to model the ο§²ow for ο§²at sewer, where the back pressure
effects can not be neglected.
Reservoirs are introduced as a water storage tanks by combining pipelines of a certain area to form a catch-
ment. This is the main simpliο§¹cation feature in the VT model and reduces the size of the real sewer network
signiο§¹cantly and thus reduces the computational effort. The simpliο§¹ed VT model is designed for control
purposes and to keep computational effort to a minimum, which becomes critical when modelling large
scale sewer networks such as the Barcelona benchmark model, in [3]. In addition, the ο§²ow in the pipes is
exclusively modeled using time delay link elements and thus no back pressure effect is included in the process.
2.2.2
Hybrid Linear Delayed Model
Methodology similar to the one used for the VT model is used for the Hybrid Linear Delayed (HLD) model,
where the main difference between the two models is the inclusion of transportation time for wastewater
ο§²owing in the pipes, of the HLD model. Time delays are added to the system process to account for the
transportation time, and switching equations6, in order to obtain a more accurate model.
As the HLD model is then able to track the system behavior with more accuracy than the VT model, the HLD
model approach becomes more relevant to be used when a speciο§¹c case study model is provided. Moreover,
the difference in performance between the two model techniques is therefore not dependent on the control
performance but on the provided details of the system. Further, the HLD approach is proven to have a fast
computation time and is an acceptable modelling approach for large scale systems. However, the HLD and
VT modelling methods can be combined for applications where accuracy is only required for parts of the
system, according to [6].
2.2.3
The Muskingum Model
The Muskingum7 model procedure is named after the river itself, for which the method was used. The model
is used by giving either the physical parameters or real data measurements. A reservoir is used as a storage
unit for the water quantity, wherein the mass balance conservation equation is used to accumulate the volume
6Which describe the logic behind weir ο§²ow and overο§²ow phenomenas, according to [6], p.73.
7The Muskingum River is located in southeastern Ohio in the United States, and approximately 178 km long.
==PAGE:34==
8
CHAPTER 2. MODELLING METHODS
quantities. Moreover, two conversion factors are used to scale the reservoir inο§²ow and outο§²ow in order to ο§¹t
the model better, according to [6]. More precisely, these two scaling factors are used to scale the average
transportation of some conditions, as further described in [10].
2.2.4
The Integrator Delay Model
The integrator delay modelling method (ID) is designed in the frequency domain and is widely known for its
simple model structure and compactness, that further enables the model to have short computation time. In
addition, the model is thus preferable for modelling and control design, where the integrator part describes
the volume level of the reservoir due to changes in the inο§²ow and outο§²ow with respect to reservoirs surface
area. The delay time is then characterized as a propagation delay that describes the gradual change from
upstream to downstream ο§²ow i.e. it determines the inο§²ow to the reservoir. Further, the ID model is known to
be a sufο§¹cient approach when modelling a canal pool in low frequency domain, if two considerations are
taken into account: relatively small changes in system behavior and the frequency of the input is below the
resonance frequency, according to [11].
Another method, introduced in [12] and [13], referred to as the integrator delay model with zero (IDZ) is
introduced and obtained by augmenting the ID model and adding a zero to the model i.e. instead of using a
mathematical delay approximation, a group delay is used that allows the IDZ model to be more applicable
for both low and high frequencies. This feature also increases accuracy in time domain simulations, as the
direct inο§²uences on the water level discharge, is accounted by the zero. Furthermore, the IDZ model can
be used for such applications as distant PI and multivariable controllers for even more advanced control
strategies.
Additionally, in [10], the Muskingum and ID modelling methods are compared for regulator applications such
as PID and predictive control strategies. For the PID approach, the ID model resulted in faster computation
time and more energy efο§¹ciency. For the predictive control approach, the Muskingum model resulted in
more energy efο§¹ciency, although reasonable results were also obtained with the ID model.
2.3
Summary
Several modelling methods were introduced in this chapter, that all include simpliο§¹cations of the Saint
Venant equations, with the purpose of ο§¹nding a preferable modelling approach for the thesis case study model.
Moreover, since real data measurements are not provided for this study, both the HLD and Muskingum
modelling methods are not considered.
Additionally, the ID and IDZ modelling methods are more suitable for river systems, where occurrences of
large variations in ο§²ows due to violent rain episodes, can be problematic for the linearized systems i.e. when
methods are implemented for sewer networks, according to [6] and are therefore not considered.
This narrows down the possibilities for applicable models, with the VT model seeming to be the most
appropriate choice and is therefore selected. Moreover, the VT model is the most frequently used modelling
method for sewer networks8.
8From previous studies in [5], [6], [3].
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 3
Control Formulation and Analysis
3.1
Introduction to Control Methods
The Linear-quadratic regulator (LQR) and Model Predictive Control (MPC) methods will be introduced
in this chapter. The control theory behind the LQR method is widely used as a basis in many modern
control procedures. Stability of the closed-loop system is guaranteed for the steady state inο§¹nite horizon
LQR feedback controller, given that the discrete system is stabilizable and observable. Moreover, the LQR
controller is designed to minimize a quadratic cost function, where its performance and quality relies on
measurements provided in [14]. The LQR method is the foundation of the MPC approach, which is an
advanced modern control technique and widely used in industrial applications, such as in the case of hybrid
systems [15],[16].
The MPC method is an optimal controller that solves the optimization problem and ο§¹nds the optimal control
signal, with the main advantage being the ability to solve the optimization problem with respect to system
boundaries and future input signals. In addition, the MPC algorithm can handle both input and output
constraints when ο§¹nding the optimal solution. This method also uses coupling between the constraints. Also
this controller is able to predict future control signals within the prediction horizon, but it only uses the ο§¹rst
one.
The prediction horizon is constant but is moved with each time step9, resulting in a moving horizon as can
be seen in ο§¹gure 3.1, where the green line is the prediction from sample k to (k + Np) and then at next step
the prediction horizon is shifted by one sample, represented by the red line from (k + 1) to (k + 1 + Np).
The control horizon can be shorter than or equal to the prediction horizon (Nc βâΛNp). Moreover, when the
prediction horizon is larger than the control horizon (Nc < Np), the prediction of the control signal is for
shorter periods of time which can lead to worse performance but also in a shorter computation time since the
QP problem becomes smaller.
It should be noted that it is problematic if MPC is used on a system with a sampling time shorter than the
required computation time, as this makes on-line control impossible[17],[18].
9Prediction horizon and the control horizon are considered to be the same in this case.
9
==PAGE:36==
10
CHAPTER 3. CONTROL FORMULATION AND ANALYSIS
Figure 3.1: Movement of prediction horizon.
The difference between normal LQR and MPC is that the LQR does not take into account the input nor
output constraints of the system. Also when solving the QP problem, the normal LQR does not predict
the future control signals for a certain horizon. Further, when the same objective function is used for both
controllers they can be compared, as LQR and unconstrained MPC controllers are the same (Unconstrained
MPC = LQR) in theory. The prediction and weighting matrices, Qw and Rw, also need to be the same for
both controllers.
It is possible to formulate the LQR to operate within system boundaries and for a certain horizon10, but
usually it is easier and more practical to use the MPC formulation when handling constraints.
3.2
Model Predictive Control
In this section, objective functions are introduced for different MPC techniques and the Quadratic Program-
ming (QP) problem is formulated for each case11. This is done systematically, starting with the least complex
method (i.e. the objective function and involvement of constraints). In addition, a compact form of the 3TC
model matrices, of eq. 4.15, is introduced for the MPC setup, where the compact model matrices hold the
system output process for a certain prediction horizon, Np. System states, inputs, outputs and boundary
conditions associated with the QP problem formulas are presented in a vector based form, where vectors are
of same lengths as the prediction horizon, Np. For all cases, the prediction and control horizon are equal such
that Np = Nc, making it possible to combine the sum of outputs and control signals, as can be seen in eq.3.13.
A block diagram of the MPC model can be seen in ο§¹gure 3.2, that further shows which signals are related to
the MPC controller and how it is implemented on the system. Further, the denoted inputs to the controller
are the reference or set-point, r, disturbance, d, and measurement outputs, y. These signals are then used
to solve the QP problem based on a speciο§¹c performance index, enabling the MPC controller to return an
optimized control signal, u. Notice that in this case, the actual and measured outputs, denoted z and y, are
the same.
10This method is sometimes referred to as the LQ tuning method with receding horizon, see Appendix E.1.
11Equations presented in this section are mostly based on literatures by J.M. Maciejowski [19] and by J. B. Rawlings & D. Q.
Mayne [20].
==PAGE:37==
3.2. MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
11
Figure 3.2: Block diagram of the MPC model
Furthermore, both feed-back and feed-forward control procedures can be implemented for the MPC formula-
tion. For each time step associated, signals in the systemβÄôs feed-back (i.e. the measurement outputs, y), are
compared to the systemβÄôs corresponding set-point, r. The error vector between y and r, will determine the
control action needed to maintain the desired performance of system. Consequently, feed-forward control is
used to feed known or estimated disturbances, that enter the system, to the controller. This leads to a more
robust system, since the controller knows which disturbances are going to inο§²uence the system and will try
to compensate for those disturbances [14].
3.2.1
Unconstrained MPC
For the unconstrained MPC case, no boundary conditions are taken into account when control action is
applied to the system. This enables the control and output signals to take on any numerical value, regardless
of the consequences to the system. Consequently, this approach is not desirable, since system elements
and equipment such as gates and pipes have structural limitations that need to be satisο§¹ed in order to avoid
system failures.
In addition, the objective function in eq. 3.1 is used to formulate the unconstrained MPC approach and
moreover to minimize the output and reference error vector. The weighted 2-norm is used when solving the
least square problem, such that a negative or positive deviation are equally undesirable for the system. Since
there is a known disturbance entering the system, the formulation will be as shown in eq. 3.1.

3.2.2
Constrained MPC
Several constrained MPC approaches are now introduced which can be categorized for input variables, u, as
constraints on their rate of change and boundary limits, and for output variables, z, as hard and softened
constraints on boundary limits. In practice, constrained MPC methods have proven to be a sufο§¹cient control
approach when handling boundary limits of a system, where the operating point of the system is often close
to the boundary and safety limits14. Moreover, since the QP problem is solved on-line for each sample, the
computation speed of the controllers will be affected, as the demand of including additional constraints to
the QP problem is increased due to mathematical complexity that follows.
The constrained MPC approaches introduced in this chapter are presented in order of complexity, starting
with the simplest one. For each approach, relevant parameters and formulas are presented and explained.
14For example, actuators have limited range of motion which can not be exceeded.
==PAGE:42==
16
CHAPTER 3. CONTROL FORMULATION AND ANALYSIS
3.2.2.1
Input Rate Constrained MPC
Linear inequality constraints are imposed on the input rates, βàÜu, of the objective function and QP problem
of eqs. 3.18 and 3.24, respectively. Further, the rate of movement constraints are added as an extra feature
to the unconstrained MPC formulation of Section 3.2.1.1. This feature can be essential in practice when
controlled devices or equipment are bounded by minimum and maximum rate limits, and if exceeded due to
the control action applied a mechanical failure can occur. Boundary conditions on the rate of movement for
the control signal, βàÜu, are formulated, where expression: βàÜu(k) = u(k) βà£u(k βà£1), is solved to be within
the linear inequality constraints.

3.2.2.2
Input Constrained MPC
Boundary conditions on system input variables are formulated in this section, using a hard constrained MPC
approach. Hard constraints can be described as the structural constraints of the system, where lower and
upper storage limits of a pipe are based on its dimensions and may not be exceeded. Thus, by using this
approach the optimal solution of the QP problem is guaranteed to be within inputs boundary limits. With
the hard linear inequality constraints formulation for the input boundaries, input constraints are added as
subjects to the objective function and QP problem descriptions, as shown in eqs. 3.27 and 3.30, respectively.
Notice that the expression of œÜ in eqs. 3.26 and 3.29 remain the same as in previous section.

by using this approach, it is ensured that system inputs, input rates and outputs, cannot exceed their lower
nor upper boundary limits.

3.2.2.4
Input and Soft Output Constrained MPC
The ο§¹nal MPC approach that is introduced in this chapter, is the input and soft output constrained MPC
approach. Hard constraints are formulated for system inputs and rates of change, and the constraints of the
output upper limits are softened15. Thus, the QP solver is allowed to obtain a solution, to some extent, that
violates the system outputs upper limits, if required. This feature adds advantage for systems that support
this approach. Whereas it is possible to get an infeasible solution of the QP problem, if only hard constraints
are implemented on the system. Thus, by adding this feature a solution of the QP problem can be obtained
that is not within the hard constrained boundaries. As an example, consider the situation of an overο§²owing
15This method is often referred to as soft MPC or slack variable technique when constraints are referred to as softened.
==PAGE:46==
20
CHAPTER 3. CONTROL FORMULATION AND ANALYSIS
water tank, where upper boundaries are exceeded. Alternatively, obtaining an appropriate behavior for slack
variables, lies in the choice of weights. Thus, with proper tuning, slack variable intensities, tendencies to
exceed the constraints can be adjusted.
The objective function for this approach is shown in eq. 3.37, where the parameter, œà, denotes the slack
variables and is weighted with the matrix P.

3.3
Summary
In this chapter, the LQR and MPC controllers were introduced and the differences between them described.
From studying differences between the two controllers, it was seen that MPC is more beneο§¹cial due to its
unique ability to include constraints.
Further, the beneο§¹ts of having feed-forward and feed-backward control was discussed and a block diagram
of the MPC model was shown in ο§¹gure 3.2. The MPC problem was derived and constraints were added one
by one, which lead to the objective function obtained in eq. 3.37, minimized by the controller when solving
the QP problem from eq. 3.44.
Lastly, the formulation of Input and Soft Output Constrained MPC will further be used for closed-loop
system analysis and referred to as the normal ISOC MPC.
==PAGE:48==
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 4
Case Study Model
4.1
Introduction
The selected modelling method from Section 2.2 is used to design a case study model, that will be referred
to as the three Tank Catchment model (3TC), from this moment on. The 3TC model consists of one real
tank and two catchments and is a preferable choice as a starting point, considering the number of tanks and
components that consists in the model. The sewer network can thus be analyzed, in order to understand
system behavior and associated components.
In addition, model components, block diagrams and relevant mathematical equations are presented and
explained. The 3TC model will be analyzed in Chapter 6 and later on augmented.
4.2
Sewer Network Components
4.2.1
Virtual Tanks
The virtual tanks are often referred to as catchments, where volume quantities from numerous pipelines are
collected together in certain areas. This simpliο§¹cation reduces the amount of system variables signiο§¹cantly,
along with the necessary computational effort, as described in Section 2.2.1. The schematic of a virtual
tank component is provided in ο§¹gure 4.1, showing the characteristics and associated parameters, where the
conservation of volume is obtained from the mass balance equation, introduced in eq. 2.1. The parameter ¬·v,
denotes the tankβÄôs maximum volume capacity limit and if it is exceeded, an overο§²ow situation will occur.
Moreover, the parameter qover, represents the overο§²ow that exceeds the tank upper boundary, which is
assumed to be diverted to another tank or directly into the environment17.
Discretization methods are applied on the Saint Venant equations to obtain a discrete time linear expression
for the stored volume in the virtual tanks18. The discrete time difference equation of the virtual tank is
presented in eq. 4.1.
v(k + 1) = v(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (qin(k) βà£qout(k) βà£qover(k))
(4.1)
The stored volume is denoted as v, the inο§²ow given as qin, the outο§²ow as qout,, the overο§²ow as qover and
the sampling time as Ts.
17These situations are explained in more details in [3].
18According to [6].
23
==PAGE:50==
24
CHAPTER 4. CASE STUDY MODEL
Figure 4.1: Schematic of virtual tank component.
The mass balance between the inο§²ow and outο§²ow of the tank, is used to calculate the volume change as
a function of time. The inο§²ow, qin, can be constructed to be the sum of several inο§²ows, as shown in eq.
4.2, and thus based on the sewer network structure and commonly categorized as manipulated variables,
disturbance inputs and outο§²ows from redirection gates. Further, the volume change is derived by using the
conversion between ο§²ow rate and volume, as the product of the system sampling time, Ts, and the difference
between the ο§²ow rates, qin,j and qout,j, as illustrated in eq. 4.1.
When an overο§²ow situation occurs, inο§²ow, qin, is larger than the outο§²ow, qout, and the tankβÄôs maximum
level, ¬·v, is exceeded. The overο§²ow is represented by qover, which becomes non-zero during overο§²ow.
The inο§²ow to the virtual tank is calculated as:
qin,j(k) = qin,1(k) + qin,2(k)+, ¬Ζ ¬Ζ ¬Ζ , +qin,n(k),
for j = 1, 2, . . . , n
(4.2)
The outο§²ow from the virtual tank is then expressed as:
qout(k) = Έ≤ ¬Ζ v(k)
(4.3)
It can be seen from eq. 4.3, that the outο§²ow is assumed to be proportional to the volume in the tank for the
linear case. This outο§²ow equation can also be obtained for the nonlinear system, where the relation between
them is: qout(k) = Έ≤ ¬Ζ
p
v(k). A more accurate approach can be formulated for the nonlinear relation, but
with the consequence of making the model more complex.
The constant Έ≤, is the volume/ο§²ow conversion coefο§¹cient [sβà£1], obtained from real data measurements and
for each tank or catchment.
4.2.2
Real Tanks
Real tanks are actual water containers, which serve an important role within a sewer network, where they
can be used as buffers to produce a slack behavior within the system. The inο§²ow and outο§²ow of the tanks
can be controlled in order to reduce the risk of overο§²ow and to maximize the performance of a WWTP. To
control ο§²ows, gates are placed at the inlet and outlet of the real tanks. As such, inο§²ow to the real tanks
can thus be increased, while the outο§²ow is reduced to force an increase in tank water level. A schematic
of the real tank and associated components, are presented in ο§¹gure 4.2, which is similar to the schematic
of the virtual tank, apart from the presence of two gates present. The inο§²ow, qin,2, is controlled by using a
redirection gate, while the retention gate is used to control the outο§²ow, qout,3.
==PAGE:51==
4.2. SEWER NETWORK COMPONENTS
25
Figure 4.2: Schematic of real tank components, with inο§²ow as an outο§²ow from the redirection gate.
The discrete time difference equation that describes the volume conservation for the real tank, is presented in
eq. 4.4,
vr(k + 1) = vr(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (qin,2(k) βà£qout,3(k) βà£qover(k))
(4.4)
where qin,2 and qout,3 are manipulated variables.
An overο§²ow device is added to the real tanks, as a safety measure19. The overο§²ow, qover, is implemented in
a same way as for the virtual tanks. The outο§²ow of the real tank is then obtained using eq. 4.3.
4.2.3
Rainfall
A rainfall is regarded as the most inο§²uential factor that affects a sewer network, where the estimated run-off
water from rainfalls are assumed to be a disturbance factor that enters the system, as presented in eq. 4.5.
Moreover, run-off water for a ο§¹nite rainfall time, is described as rain water that accumulates after reaching
the ground. Therefore, many factors need to be taken into account to determine the run-off process for
a given area and rainstorm episode (e.g. rain intensity, soil type, episode duration and how the rain is
distributed). Thus, a complex modelling procedure is necessary when an accurate model of a run-off process
is required.
Further, a general approach of introducing a disturbance input for this modelling technique is expressed in
eq. 4.5,
d(k) = S ¬Ζ œï ¬Ζ It(k)
(4.5)
where S[m2], denotes the surface area of the catchment, œï, the ground absorption coefο§¹cient and It[m/s],
the rain intensity. The absorption factor needs to be derived for each catchment due to the rainfall water that
is absorbed by the ground and is based on a real data measurements, further explained in [21].
19For situations where the real tank full capacity is demanded.
==PAGE:52==
26
CHAPTER 4. CASE STUDY MODEL
Furthermore, equipment used for rain forecasts such as weather radars and rainfall gauges are used to
determine (estimate) the intensity of the incoming rain. These measurements are then used for calculating
estimated rain intensities (magnitudes), where the accuracy of the estimations are based on the uncertainty
of the forecasts.
4.2.4
Gates
Two types of gates are introduced in this section, referred to as the redirection and retention gates. These
gates add variability within the sewer network, where ο§²ows in pipes can be distributed to the sewer network
depending on demand and are thus added for control purposes.
Redirection Gates
This type of gate is used to control the inο§²ow rates to connected pipes by changing the direction of the ο§²ow
through the gate, as ο§¹gure 4.3 and eq. 4.6 illustrates. The inο§²ow, qin,1, to the redirection gate is divided into
two outο§²ows qout,1 and qout,2, proportionally and their ο§²ow rates thus controlled.
Figure 4.3: Schematic of redirection gates component.
Further, redirection gates can be placed anywhere in the sewer network, where the most commonly used
locations are before tanks. In practice, local controllers are often used to add these characteristics in sewer
networks.
qin(k) = qout,1(k) + qout,2(k)
(4.6)
qout,i βâΛqout,i(k) βâΛqout,i
for i = 1, 2
(4.7)
Notice that redirection gates can be implemented as binary logic gates or for continuous time. The approach
chosen to be used in the modelling procedure for this thesis, is the continuous one, where gate outο§²ows can
vary between the minimum and maximum capacity of connected pipes, qout,i and qout,i, as illustrated by the
linear inequality constraints equation, in eq. 4.7.
Retention Gates
In practice, retention gates are used to control tank outο§²ows and are controlled by using local controllers.
The beneο§¹t of using retention gates is maintaining wastewater ο§²ow at a certain point in the sewer network,
as eq. 4.8 indicates. A preferable choice for the location of a retention gate, for this modelling approach, is
==PAGE:53==
4.2. SEWER NETWORK COMPONENTS
27
after a real tank. Moreover, as virtual tanks can only use redirection gates, since the virtual tanks are not
able to block their outlet completely.
qin(k) = qout(k)
(4.8)
qout βâΛqout(k) βâΛqout
(4.9)
Flows to and from retention gates are limited by the connected pipe structure as well and thus must be within
the linear inequality constraints shown in eq. 4.9.
4.2.5
Weir Overο§²ow Device (Nodes)
These devices are divided into two categories: splitting and merging nodes. Redirection gates are similar
to splitting nodes, where inο§²ow to the node is split into multiple outο§²ows. Conversely, several inο§²ows are
merged into a combined outο§²ow in merging nodes.
The ο§²ow equation for splitting nodes:
qin(k) = qout(k) + qover(k),
(4.10)
where the inο§²ow into a node is qin, the outο§²ow is qout, and the wastewater ο§²owing over the weir is denoted
as qover.
The ο§²ow equation for merging nodes is:
qin,i(k) + qin,i+1(k) + ¬Ζ ¬Ζ ¬Ζ + qin,n(k) = qout(k),
for i = 1, 2, . . . , n
(4.11)
where inο§²ows are denoted as qin,i and n represents number of inο§²ows to a node, that forms the outο§²ow, qout.
4.2.6
Level Gauge
Level gauges are sensors, often referred to as limnimeters, that measure the volume level or ο§²ow rate at a
certain point in the sewer network. For the control of a water storage system such as a sewer network, these
sensors become a necessity. Further, level gauges can be included in system structures where it is preferable
for control efforts, knowledge of quantities and/or ο§²ow rates.
4.2.7
Wastewater Treatment Plant
One of the main objectives of a sewer network, is to minimize environmental contamination due to sewage
water being released into the environment. Therefore, it is desirable to direct the sewage water to a
wastewater treatment plant (WWTP), passing through the process of removing physical, chemical, and
biological contaminants from the wastewater, before being released back into the environment, usually into
the sea or a river. The wastewater will continue to be directed to the plant until the maximum capacity of the
plant is reached.
==PAGE:54==
28
CHAPTER 4. CASE STUDY MODEL
4.3
Three Tank Catchment Model
The original model represents the Barcelona sewer network, which has been shown to be a preferable choice
for a benchmark model of a sewer network in several studies. Further, the 3TC model is only a part of the
total Barcelona model structure, which MartΟ≠nez [3]20 has used for other applications as well.
4.3.1
System Description
A schematic of the 3TC model is shown in ο§¹gure 4.4a and constructed by using components from ο§¹gure
4.4b. One real tank and two virtual tanks, denoted as Ti,R and Ti,Vj, are included in the model, where i
represents the i-th number of tank and j the j-th virtual tank.
The 3TC model is presented as a ο§¹rst order discrete time system with sampling time, Ts. The system states,
x, and measured outputs, y, are the stored volume levels in the 3TC model, as can be seen from eqs. 4.12
and 4.16. The control variables in u, are the three ο§²ows denoted u1, u2 and u3, seen in ο§¹gure 4.4a. Control
effort is applied to the ο§²ow rates in the pipes, in order to achieve a desired set-point or volume levels in
the tanks. The ο§²ow rates are generally obtained from the change in volume, given a time interval of sensor
measurements as previously discussed in Section 4.2.6.
(a) Three tank model.
(b) Sewer network components.
Figure 4.4: The three tank model block diagram and sewer network components.
20Chapter 8.4, p. 167.
==PAGE:55==
4.3. THREE TANK CATCHMENT MODEL
29
Additionally, three rainfall areas are included in the 3TC model and denoted d1, d2 and d3. The rainfall
areas are represented as external inο§²ows to the virtual tanks and referred to as input disturbance, d, to the
system (as d2 and d3 have been implemented to the model). For d1, a special case is constructed, where the
rainfall area is located above redirection gate, G1, as seen from ο§¹gure 4.4a and expressed in eqs. 4.13a.
Further, for this special case, d1 becomes an additional input ο§²ow to the constant wastewater inο§²ow, qc, that
is present in the sewer at all times. Moreover, the choice of value for the constant wastewater inο§²ow is a
calibrating factor and the basis for obtaining the steady state values for the system (where the mass balance
equation holds and means that the total inο§²ow to the system is equal to the total outο§²ow from the system).
Thus, the total inο§²ow to the system is distributed to each pipe and the steady state values for both tanks and
pipes are determined.
The constant wastewater inο§²ow is chosen to be qc = 7 m3/s, and the steady state values for the system (i.e.
state, measured output and manipulated variables), along with ο§²ows of associated pipes from redirection
gates and WWTP, are provided in tables 4.1, 4.2 and 4.3.
There are two types of gates used in the 3TC model, a redirection gate and a retention gate. Their location
and component types are shown in ο§¹gure 4.4, where the two redirection gates are labeled as G1 and G2, and
the retention gate as G3. In addition, the inο§²ow to redirection gate G1 are the combination of inο§²ows qc and
the input disturbance d1. The inο§²ow to redirection gate G2, is the outο§²ow from tank T2,V 2 and is redirected
to pipes u2 and q23. The retention gate, located between the tanks T2,R, and T4,V 3, is used to control the
outο§²ow from the real tank and the inο§²ow to the virtual tank, u3, as illustrated in eq. 4.8.
Notice that as the outο§²ow from the real tank is a controlled variable and structurally constrained due to the
retention gate, the outο§²ow from the real tank is larger than or equal to the control signal, u3, according to [3].
4.3.2
Linear Mathematical Model Representation
The discrete time difference equations of the 3TC model are now introduced and used to obtain the systems
state space model.
The tanksβÄô geometric equations of the 3TC model, are presented in eq. 4.12. The stored volumes of the two
virtual tanks are denoted as T2,V 2 and T4,V 3, and T3,R, for the real tank. The two inο§²ows, u1 and d2, are
combined inο§²ows to virtual tank T2,V 2 from a merging node, where u1 is a controlled variable, while d2 is
an external inο§²ow to the tank, (representing the rain magnitude for the tankβÄôs rainfall area).
For virtual tank T4,V 3, four inο§²ows are merged into a node, forming the total inο§²ow to the tank (i.e. the
control variable u3, disturbance input d3, and ο§²ows in pipes q13 and q23).
T2,V 2(k + 1) = T2,V 2(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (u1(k) + d2(k) βà£qout,2(k))
T3,R(k + 1) = T3,R(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (u2(k) βà£u3(k))
T4,V 3(k + 1) = T4,V 3(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (u3(k) + d3(k) + q13(k) + q23(k) βà£qout,4(k))
(4.12)
The outο§²ows of the virtual tanks, T2,V 2 and T4,V 3, denoted qout,2 and qout,4, respectively, are obtained by
using eq. 4.14 and the corresponding conversion coefο§¹cient Έ≤βÄôs values from table 4.1.
Remember that the controlled input ο§²ow, u3, to virtual tank T4,V 3, is the outο§²ow from the real tank, T3,R.
The equations for pipe, q13 and q23, ο§²ows are expressed as:
q13(k) = d1(k) βà£u1(k)
(4.13a)
q23(k) = qout,2(k) βà£u2(k)
(4.13b)

expressed as:
qout,i(k) = Έ≤i ¬Ζ Vj(k),

remember as mentioned in Section 3.2.1, that the disturbance, d, to the system is considered to be known,
unless declared otherwise.
4.3.4
System Response and Analysis
In this section, robustness analyses are performed on the 3TC linear model, by performing several closed-
loop simulations for the designed model and with different conο§¹gurations, where hard input and soft output
constrained MPC is formulated for the controlled input signal. Notice that the 3TC system is marginally
stable in open-loop, as the real tank eigenvalue is placed on the unit circle, as can be noticed from system
state matrix, A, from eq. 4.17. Since stability is not guaranteed when using MPC, robustness analyses
are performed on the closed-loop system. Results from three simulations will be presented, with set-point
changes made on tanks levels one tank at a time. This is done in order to gain better knowledge of the system
behavior, as the volume levels in tanks and ο§²ow rates in pipes are inο§²uencing each other.
For implementation of control, it is investigated whether the 3TC discrete time system is controllable and
22Analysis performed to evaluate the sampling time chosen for the system, is provided in Appendix B.2.
==PAGE:58==

using eq. 4.20.

The controllability matrix, Mc, is computed as a (3Ο½9) dimensional matrix with rank 3. Since the number of
rows is equal to its rank, the discrete time system is reachable and therefore controllable.
The observability matrix, Mo, is then obtained and computed as a (9Ο½3) dimensional matrix with rank 3.
Again, as the number of columns is equal to its rank and the discrete time system is observable as well.
Three closed-loop simulation are performed and results are presented in ο§¹gures 4.5, 4.6 and 4.7. The system
is simulated for duration time of 10 hours for each simulation 23. A reference change for each tank, using an
pulse step active for the time interval, 190 βâΛt βâΛ250 min, is carried out with a step size of approximately
5% of the tanksβÄô steady state values.
The tank responses from the ο§¹rst simulation are shown in ο§¹gure 4.5a, where a reference change is made on
tank T2,V 2. It can be seen that tank T2,V 2 begins to respond to the reference change, even before it occurs.
This behavior is to be expected as this effect is included in the MPC formulation and is directly inο§²uenced
by the control horizon (i.e. as the volume level in tank T4,V 3 starts to fall, the volume levels in tanks T3,R
and T2,V 2 start to rise). Moreover, it is seen that the volume of tank T2,V 2 is not able to reach the set-point
volume within the pulse interval due to system dynamics and is affected by the controller weighting as
set-points for all tanks are included in the cost function. It is also noticed that while the pulse is active on
tank T2,V 2, the volume level of the real tank, T3,R, falls and occurs as no additional wastewater is entering
the system 24. Notice that this affects the real tank more than virtual tank T4,V3, as more performance is
requested for tank T4,V3 due to weights chosen for closed-loop system.
The corresponding control signals are shown in ο§¹gure 4.5b, which shows the applied control action needed
in order to achieve the tanks set-point levels. When the controller notices the pulse, the control signals in u,
start to respond to the change in the system. The inο§²ow and outο§²ow, to and from, the real tank are controlled
by control signals u2 and u3, respectively. Thus, in response to the reference step, u2 and u3 "cooperate" in
order to optimize the performance by lowering the inο§²ow and outο§²ow of the real tank. Further, the steady
state error in the two control signals are to be expected as an offset-free approach is not included in the
objective function25.
Additionally, ο§²ows in pipes q13, q23 and ones associated with tank outο§²ows are shown, in ο§¹gures 4.5d
and 4.5c, respectively. Notice that the ο§²ow rates for outο§²ows qout,2 and qout,4 have a behavior similar to
the corresponding tanks in ο§¹gure 4.5a, which is expected due to the mass balance equation. Further, the
structural constraint present due to the retention gate at the real tankβÄôs outlet, ensures that qout,3 = u3, as can
further be seen in ο§¹gure 4.5c, where qout,3 and u3 are plotted in the same graph for comparison and shows
that they are the same.

As an overall conclusion for the three simulations, no abnormal behavior was observed from the closed-loop
system responses. The volume level signals responded to the set-point change applied for corresponding
signal, which return to their steady state values after the pulse signal have passed. The offset-error between
volume level signals and set-point levels is considered to be a normal behavior, which is caused by several
factors. Firstly, only the constant wastewater inο§²ow, qc, is entering the system, which makes the system
capable to operate in steady state. Thus, if a volume level change is made on one tank, it will affect the
volume level of other tanks, due to the redirection and retention gates. The weighting of tanks inο§²uences
the solution as well, since the objective function minimizes the absolute difference between system outputs
and set-points. Notice that wastewater that enters the system will eventually ο§²ow through tank T4,V 3, and
therefore more performance is demanded of this tank and a smaller off-sett achieved when a pulse is applied
==PAGE:61==
4.4. THE NONLINEAR SYSTEM
35
on it. Moreover, simulations where weighting affects analyses are carried out, are shown in Section 5.2.
Lastly, from observations of the three simulations, the closed-loop system is concluded to be asymptotically
stable and thus the poles are located inside the unit circle.
4.4
The Nonlinear System
In this section formulations are presented in order to obtain a nonlinear model, based on the 3TC linear
model26. This approach is opposite to the general order, which is to obtain a linear model given a nonlinear
one. The motivation for proceeding in this direction, is being able to evaluate the control performance on
the nonlinear system and for proper simulation routine setup. Remember that the 3TC case study model,
introduced in Section 4 as a discrete time linear model, is based on a small part of the Barcelona tank
catchment model in [3]. There it is stated, that the relation between the linear and nonlinear systems are
obtained from their outο§²ow equations, presented in eqs. 4.14 and 4.21, where the linear-nonlinear model are
related by the square root of the tanksβÄô volumes. This relation is shown in ο§¹gure 4.8, where the square root
function, f(x) = βàöx, is plotted, along with the function, f(x) = x, that shows the relation mentioned when
the square root function is linearized around the systemβÄôs operating point. In addition, by applying linear
approximation of f(x) = βàöx, function, f(x) = 0.5(x + 1), is obtained and it is seen that it ο§¹ts the square
root function better.

although the nonlinear signals continue to increase, resulting in a large deviation between the linear and
nonlinear open-loop processes. The average difference between the linear and open-loop volume processes

Another approach is then performed in an attempt to counter the large deviation of the tanksβÄô volume levels
and to achieve better tracking of the nonlinear open-loop system. This approach involves a time varying
model, where the nonlinear open-loop system is linearized at each sample of the simulation, using Taylor
expansion to approximate the volume geometric expressions of eq. 4.12. This is expressed in a general form
in eq. 4.22, with reference to a virtual tankβÄôs volume,

A closed-loop simulation is now performed with the same speciο§¹cations as before. The main purpose of this

28Where the LTV state matrix, At, from eq. 4.25 is used when obtaining the MPC gain matrices, as can be observed from

closed-loop simulation. In addition, the normal ISOC MPC formulation from Section 3.2.2.4 is included for
this evaluation. Thus, the control signals are obtained from the linear model, and are then applied to the
nonlinear process.
The system responses from the closed-loop simulation can be seen in ο§¹gure 4.11, where it is noticed that
the system behavior is changed, compared to the open-loop simulations. The system performance is now
optimized with respect to the objective function speciο§¹ed in eq. 3.37 due to the weighing on inputs and
outputs.
Finally, based on the system responses the time varying approach is estimating the nonlinear process quite
well. The average difference between the linear and nonlinear outο§²ow signals qout,2 and qout,4 is now
approximately 0.71% and 0.82%, respectively. Also, if these results are compared to the average deviation
when using the time varying approach, they are close to being the same. With regards to tanksβÄô volume,
it is seen that the average deviation between the linear and nonlinear volume signals, T2,V 2 and T4,V 3, is
approximately 2.97% and 0.63%, respectively, and compared to the open-loop case, even better. Same can
be concluded for the ο§²ow signal of pipe q23 where the average difference between the linear and nonlinear
signals is 0.95%, which is similar to the open-loop case.
4.5
Summary
In this chapter, the selected modelling method concepts and components were introduced and explained. For
the modelling part, the methods methodology was used to achieve the benchmark model, 3TC, based on the
Barcelona benchmark model. The 3TC modelβÄôs mathematical equations were constructed analytically and
then further used to obtain a discrete time state space model of the system. The numerical values of system
parameters such as steady state and boundaries were then introduced. Controllability and observability
were then analyzed showing that matrix pairs (A,B) and (A,C) were controllable and observable, respectively.
Further, the closed-loop system stability was evaluated through simulation, as the open-loop system is
marginally stable due to the real tank. The closed-loop description was simulated and the system response
analyzed for different simulation routines. Furthermore, the 3TC system is concluded to be stable as
no abnormal behavior were observed from the robustness analysis, and thus the modelling procedure is
considered successful.
Additionally, in order to compensate for the steady state error of the control signals (ο§¹gures 4.5b, 4.6b
and 4.7b), then by modifying the cost function to include a reference for control signal, u, this can be
compensated for29.
In Section 4.4, the linear-nonlinear open-loop systems were analyzed, with the objective of achieving a
linear modelling approach that estimates the nonlinear system sufο§¹ciently. Desirable results were achieved
by using the time varying approach, where linearizing points are updated for each step of simulation.
Considering that the linear system provided by MartΟ≠nez [4], is a good approximation of the nonlinear
system, it is decided to proceed with the linear model by MartΟ≠nez, when analyzing the system further. Thus
the time varying approach is not considered.
Finally, a closed-loop simulation was performed using the ISOC MPC, validating how well the linear system
is following the nonlinear one. The results from the time varying open- and closed-loop simulation seemed
to be sufο§¹cient. It is concluded that results are dependent on the circumstances simulated.
29The associating method for these modiο§¹cations are provided in [19].
==PAGE:67==
4.5. SUMMARY
41
In Appendix B.3, closed-loop simulations are presented, where the responses of the nonlinear, time varying
and time invariant systems are analyzed further.

==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 5
Implementation and Evaluation
5.1
Introduction
In this chapter, implementation of several aspects such as system and control formulation, are evaluated
and presented. Firstly, a weight tuning strategy for the performance index and normal slack variables is
introduced along with weighting of the scalar slack variable. Moreover, different weighting approaches are
analyzed in order to evaluate how weights inο§²uence the system.
In addition, different types of constraints within the system are examined for the purpose of control
formulation and simulation. The necessary calculations are performed and the linear inequality equations
for constraints are presented along with associated numerical values, which will be used for all closed-loop
simulations.
Further, the unconstrained MPC and ο§¹nite LQR approaches are compared, as in theory these two approaches
are the same. Moreover, the same objective function is used in the design of both controllers and through
closed-loop simulations, their responses compared.
Lastly, a fast MPC method, referred to as scalar MPC, is introduced with the main purpose of simplifying
the optimization process for the controller, in order to reduce its computation effort. The fast MPC method
is applied with the ISOC MPC approach. Thus, modiο§¹cations will be performed on associated equations
from Section 3.2.2.4.
5.2
Weighting Matrices and Tuning
In order to control and achieve desirable performance of a system, weighting matrices, Qw and Rw, need to
be tuned, when the system outputs and inputs are weighted, respectively. The general properties that need
to be considered when tuning the controller is disturbance rejection and reference tracking, along with the
robust performance.
Further, as stated in eq. 5.1, weighting matrix, Qw, needs to be positive semideο§¹nite and, Rw, needs to be
positive deο§¹nite, in order for the control problem to have an optimal solution.
Qw(k) βâΞ0, βàÄk
Rw(k) > 0, βàÄk
(5.1)
A well known method, BrysonβÄôs rule, is used as a starting point when tuning weighting matrices, where
weights are calculated as shown in eq. 5.2. Further, Qw and Rw are assumed to be diagonal[14], where n
43
==PAGE:70==
44
CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION AND EVALUATION
and m are the numbers of outputs and control inputs, respectively.
Qw(k)ii =
1
max(zi(k)2)
for i = 1, 2, . . . n
Rw(k)jj =
1
max(uj(k)2)
for j = 1, 2, . . . m
(5.2)
Further tuning is then applied by scaling the weighting matrices for desired performance, usually done by
trial and error. This process can be very time consuming and does not guarantee the expected performance.
Furthermore, the system state is driven rather quickly to the origin, when weighting matrix, Qw, is adjusted
to be larger than Rw, as the control action is increased on the system. Subsequently, when Rw is larger
than Qw, then the control action is reduced and the state will be driven to the origin more slowly [20].
Consequently, several simulations are performed in order to analyze this behavior of the 3TC model, where
different weights are chosen for Qw and two special cases introduced. Three simulations are performed for
each case and for the closed-loop system. A reference step is applied on one tank at a time and with different
weight for the corresponding output.
For the ο§¹rst case, a reference change is made on tank T2,V 2 using weighting matrices, Qw and Rw, from eq.
5.3. Thus, for the ο§¹rst simulation, Qw and Rw remain unchanged, but then corresponding output weight for
tank T2,V 2 is changed so that Qw,1 βâΛQw βâΛQw,2
30. Then, similar approach is used for output weighting
on tank T4,V 3, for the second simulation31. The system responses are shown in ο§¹gure 5.1.

5.3
Implementation of Constraints
This section provides a declaration of the system constraints, where their properties are analyzed (i.e.
types of constraints and their involvement in system process). Moreover, guidelines for steps taken when
implementing constraints on a system description and for control formulation are presented and explained.
Usually implemented constraints are constants, where for the 3TC system, boundaries need to be evaluated
at each time step of the simulation (constraints on control signals), due to the redirection and retention
gates, categorized as structural constraints. However, control rates, βàÜu, and outputs, z, are implemented as
constant constraints for the 3TC model. The constraints on the control rates, βàÜu, limit how much signals are
allowed to change from one time sample to the next. Moreover, since constraints on control rates are given
as deviation variables, the numerical values of lower boundaries need to be smaller than zero, while upper
ones are larger than zero. Numerical values for the rates are shown in table 5.5 and the implementation of

5.4
Comparison of Control Implementation
Now, control design methods are compared in order to ensure that the MPC formulation from Chapter 3, is
correct. A ο§¹nite LQR optimal controller is designed and its performance compared to the unconstrained
MPC approach, as both are optimal controllers and solve the quadratic programming problem. The main
difference between them, is that the classical LQR controller solves the optimization for an inο§¹nite time,
while the MPC uses a moving prediction horizon when solving the optimization. Thus, a correct MPC
formulation can be conο§¹rmed by comparing the performance to an LQR. The objective function used for

5.4.1
LQR Formulation
In this section, equations associated with the ο§¹nite LQR controller design are presented. The main concept
behind this approach is to compute and update the LQR controller gain for each step of the simulation.
This is done by solving the Riccati equation, given in eq. 5.15, for each sample within the control horizon,
solving the equation backwards in time. As a starting point the sample horizon, N, is equal to k and iterated
backwards to k = 1. The Riccati solution matrix, X, is initialized using the reference and output weighting
matrix, Qw, of eq. 5.3 such that X(k = N) = Qw.
The discrete time algebraic Riccati equation:
0 = AT ¬Ζ X ¬Ζ A βà£X βà£(AT ¬Ζ X ¬Ζ B) ¬Ζ (BT ¬Ζ X ¬Ζ B + Rw)βà£1 ¬Ζ (BT ¬Ζ X ¬Ζ A) + Qw
(5.15)
The equations that where used for the Riccati solution and LQR gain update, KLQR, in the ο§¹nite LQR
algorithm are presented in eqs. 5.16 and 5.17. In addition, the associated matrices, A and B, are the state and
input matrices, presented in eq. 4.17.
X(k) = AT ¬Ζ X(k + 1) ¬Ζ A βà£(AT ¬Ζ X(k + 1) ¬Ζ B) ¬Ζ (BT ¬Ζ X(k + 1) ¬Ζ B + R)βà£1
¬Ζ (BT ¬Ζ X(k + 1) ¬Ζ A) + Qw,
(5.16)
where X(k) is unknown.

obtain these values is presented in eq. 5.18, and are then used to obtain the control signal, u, for the system,

5.5
Scalar MPC Technique
The normal ISOC MPC approach from Section 3.2.2.4, was formulated in order to soften the system outputs
and implemented as additions to the output constraints for each time step of the prediction horizon. This
can be problematic in practice as associated matrices are extended, with the consequence of increasing the
computation effort, when solving the QP problem.
Consequently, the main purpose of introducing the scalar MPC technique is to simplify associated mathe-
matical terms, in order to reduce the computational effort. The main difference between these techniques, is
that the scalar technique uses only one slack variable, added to the output constraints and for only one time
step in the prediction horizon. This can reduce ο§²exibility of a desired performance for a system, as slack
variables for all outputs are weighted the same38.
The simpliο§¹cation lies in reducing the sizes of associated matrices. Further, recursion of the scalar MPC
method are shown by reformulating expressions from Section 3.2.2.4. In addition, dimensions of associated
matrices of the scalar and normal MPC methods are shown in table 5.6, in order to examine the reduction
introduced by formulating the scalar MPC. Parameters nu and nz, are denoted as number of inputs and
outputs of the system, respectively.
The corresponding matrix parameters of the normal ISOC MPC from table 5.6, are reformulated for the
scalar MPC technique in eqs. 5.23, 5.24 and 5.25. Starting by reformulating the Hessian and Gramian
matrices Υ€H and Υ€g from eq. 3.41. For Υ€H, the compact weighting matrix Υ€P, formulated to weigh slack variables
for each output, at each time step in the prediction horizon, is replaced with a single weighting variable. The
denoted parameter, p, is the slack variable weight and a single scalar. Similar changes are then applied to
38See eq. 5.4, where different weights are used for slack variables of the normal MPC.
==PAGE:78==
52
CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION AND EVALUATION
Matrix / Dimensions
Normal ISOC
Scalar ISOC
Υ€H
2¬Ζ Np¬Ζ nz Ο½ 2¬Ζ Np¬Ζ nz
nz¬Ζ Np + 1 Ο½ Np¬Ζ nz + 1
Υ€g
2¬Ζ Np¬Ζ nz Ο½ 1

5.6
Summary
In this chapter, tuning and evaluation of numerical values of weighting matrices from Section 5.2 was carried
out. Although this process can be time consuming, it is still a necessary procedure as weights have great
inο§²uence on system performance. As illustrated in ο§¹gure 5.1, a large change in system response was noticed
and a faster system obtained when using larger weights for Qw. Moreover, in the time interval where the
pulse signal was active on tank, T4,V 3, (ο§¹gure 5.1b) a offset error occurred, along with a large deviation
in tanks T2,V 2 and T3,R. This behavior results from a trade-off in the objective function, as there is no
disturbance and thus no additional ο§²ow entering the system. Thus, the existing wastewater in the system is
39Notice that vector of ones and zeros are denoted as 1 and 0, respectively.
==PAGE:79==
5.6. SUMMARY
53
therefore distributed to the tanks and the quantity going to each tank, depends on the weight applied for each
tank.
Furthermore, the selected weighting matrices and weigh that will be used when further analyzing the
closed-loop system, were presented in eqs. 5.3, 5.4 and 5.5.
Only constant constraints for system boundary limits were not an option, due to the equipment used to
control the system (i.e. pipes associated with the redirection and retention gates). Thus, all appropriate
methods for calculating the constraints in the system were presented in Section 5.3, including the inequality
for associated pipes which can be implemented on-line. This provides the controller with correct knowledge
of pipe capacity at any given time in simulation and more importantly, ensures that constraints are not vi-
olated. Further, equations updated for the controller input constraints are provided in eqs. 5.10, 5.11 and 5.12.
Then, comparison of unconstrained MPC and LQR controllers were performed in Section 5.4, in order to
ensure that implementation of the MPC were correct, as these methods should show equal results. Given this
statement, it was ensured that the same speciο§¹cations were provided for both control methods Subsequently,
a closed-loop simulation was performed and the system responses using both methods compared. The results
of this comparison showed that the responses from the MPC and LQR methods were identical when a step
was applied on the system (shown in ο§¹gure 5.4). It was then concluded that the implementation of the MPC
method was done correctly.
Lastly, a scalar MPC technique was introduced in Section 5.5 and modiο§¹ed for the ISOC MPC method.
Additionally, steps were taken in order to reduce mathematical complexity, compared to the normal MPC
approach. The reduced matrices were shown in table 5.6, where the reduced complexity is clearly seen. The
scalar approach also reduces the computational time, enhancing controller performance compared to the
normal approach. Thus, reducing computational effort is of key interest.
==PAGE:80==
==PAGE:81==
CHAPTER 6
Three Tank Catchment Model Results
6.1
Introduction
In this chapter, several analyses of the closed-loop 3TC systemβÄôs are carried out40. The system performance
in minimizing overο§²ow situations and maximizing treated wastewater will be evaluated, along with the
reduction of computational effort and how it affects the system performance41.
Firstly, the simulation time, tCPU, of the general MPC methods (introduced in Chapter 3) are compared for
different simulation circumstances.
Then, analyses are performed of the normal and scalar ISOC MPC methods where the system performances
are compared between methods. The inο§²uence of using different control horizon for the normal and scalar
methods are evaluated as well.
Further, the 3TC system, when using only the normal MPC method as a control strategy, is evaluated by
simulating violent and average rainfall episodes. In addition, the effect of simulating an unknown disturbance
on the 3TC system is analyzed.
Note: The computation time it takes the QP solver to obtain a solution for each step of simulation is
distinguished by the parameter, tQP, and the time it takes the computer to run the main simulation loop, by the
parameter, tCPU. Further, speciο§¹cations of the computer system used for carrying out simulations is provided
in Appendix F.
6.2
Comparison of MPC Methods
Now, six sets of conditions for simulation process are of the closed-loop simulations 42:
Case 1 Only a constant wastewater ο§²ow enters the system for a simulation time of 10 hours, resulting in a
total volume quantity of 252,000 m3.
Case 2 A rainstorm is simulated on the disturbance input, d3, consisting of ο§¹ve intense pulses, as can be
seen in ο§¹gure 6.1a. This results in a total volume quantity of 525,300 m3 delivered to the system for
a simulation time of 10 hours.
Case 3 Only a constant wastewater ο§²ow enters the system for a simulation time of 50 hours, resulting in a
total volume quantity of 1,260,000 m3 delivered to the system.

6.2.1
Unconstrained and Different Constrained MPC
Cases 1, 5 and 6, now are simulated in order to compare elapsed time, tCPU, of the main simulation loop for
different MPC approaches: UC, IC, IIRC, OC, IOC , ISOC43 and rainstorm episodes. As the MPC methods
become more complex, one will assume that the elapsed time of simulations, tCPU, becomes longer.
A chart of the elapsed times of simulations are shown in ο§¹gure 6.2, where it is seen that the elapsed time,
tCPU, increases as constraints are added to the QP problem and also as inο§²ows to the system are increased.
A large difference is noticed between elapsed times of IOC and ISOC. This is a consequence of obtaining
larger dimensions of associated controller matrices for slack variable approach. Also, it is noticed that there
is a smaller difference between the elapsed time in case 1 and 5 for the ISOC MPC method.
Other simulations were performed for the mentioned MPC approaches as well, which can be seen in
Appendix B.5.

Comparison of the normal and scalar ISOC MPC approaches is now carried out. Simulation of cases 1 to 4

method is more beneο§¹cial. For instance, if a real application of a large scale sewer network is considered (a
more complicated system), problems may occur as solutions for controlled variables become obsolete, as the
optimization is too time consuming. Thus, it is not possible to provide the solution within the sampling time
of the system.

horizon, Nc, of 24 and 6 samples or reducing the control horizon from 2 hours to 1/2 hour are considered.
Further, from a mathematical point of view, the reduction of dimensions of associated matrices of the QP
problem, for different control horizons and MPC approaches, is shown from table B.1 in Appendix B.5.

The trade-off from choosing a shorter control horizon, is clearly noticed when the performance in minimizing

volume rises for both methods. Notice that there is a larger difference in overο§²ow volume when using the
scalar MPC method, where it increases from 0.26% to 6.08%, when the control horizon is shortened. This
behavior is not surprising since the controller has less knowledge about future predictions, achieving faster
computation time, but at the cost of reducing system performance44.

the system.
6.3
System Response Analyses
The system behavior and performance is now analyzed when different disturbances are simulated for the
system. Rainstorm episodes are simulated for the disturbances in d, as pulse step signals with a constant
magnitude for different time intervals. The magnitudes of the pulse steps distinguish between types of
rainstorm simulated. These magnitudes depend on the catchment surface area, absorption factor and rain
intensities, and categorized as light, moderate, heavy and violent rainfalls. The methodology behind obtaining
these rainfalls are presented in Appendix A46. Lastly, a simulation duration of 20 h (sample horizon of 241
samples) is used.
6.3.1

magnitudes of 6, 3 and 12.5 m3/s for disturbances d1, d2 and d3, respectively. The disturbance signals are
shown in ο§¹gure 6.4.

that it takes the QP solver approximate 0.11s, at the most, to solve the QP problem, which should not be a
problem in our case since the system sampling time is 300s.
In order to analyze the overall performance of the system, factors such as the total overο§²ow volume (the
quantity of wastewater that the WWTP can handle), the volume quantity that exceeds the WWTP capacity
(thus directed to sea), and the evolution of the elapsed time, tQP, of the QP solver are evaluated by using
numerical data and ο§¹gures, gathered in table 6.5 and ο§¹gure 6.6.

The system performance is evaluated from numerical data from simulations shown in tables 6.8, 6.9 and

For cases 3TCa and 3TCc (tables 6.8 and 6.9), it is observed that there is a very small difference in the total
volume quantity directed to WWTP and that received by the sea (Sea vs. (inο§²ow-overο§²ow)), between cases.
The slightly better performance of case 3TCc is obtained, since the 3TC state and control processes have full
knowledge of the disturbances entering the system.
When the system performances in cases 3TCa and 3TCc are compared to case 3TCb (tables 6.8 and 6.9 to
6.10), it is seen that more wastewater is treated and less is received by the sea. This is due to the magnitude
of disturbances simulated for the 3TCb case, being smaller than ones simulated for cases 3TCa and 3TCc.
Remember that all disturbance signals are simulated in order to achieve the same amount of total inο§²ow to
the system, as tables 6.8, 6.9 and 6.10, verify.
The results obtained from simulations are considered sufο§¹cient for the 3TC system performance, when there
is an uncertainty in the disturbance entering the system. It is then concluded that the 3TC system can handle
uncertainty of this kind (given the circumstances simulated).
