6.4
Summary
In this chapter, analyses were conducted to evaluate system performance and elapsed times of simulations
and compared for different MPC methods and circumstances. Firstly, for the comparison between the
normal and scalar MPC methods, an interesting result were obtained from case 2 and 4, as the simpliο§¹cation
made for the scalar MPC, showed approximately 30% difference between the simulation duration times
for both cases. The overο§²ow volume only increased by 0.02% in case 2 with no change in case 4. Notice,
in order to cause the overο§²ow situations, the simulated disturbances included intense pulse signals. Thus,
the effect on the computational effort when the system is operating around boundaries can be analyzed.
As the QP solver tends to be more time consuming when the changes are detected in the slack variable signals.
From the closed-loop simulations, the normal and scalar MPC methods were evaluated for different control
horizons. The trade-off between computation time and performance of the system were well noticed, show-
ing that for shorter control horizon, faster computation time is achieved, but at the cost of system performance.
Closed-loop simulations were performed in Section 6.3 in order to analyze system performance. The system
performance was compared for average and violent rainstorm episodes. Comparison showed a difference
in total inο§²ow of only approximately 10% between average and violent rainstorm episodes. The violent
rainstorm affected system performance more than the average one, since the difference in the total volume
directed to sea is around 14%. Results also showed a stable response of the system.
==PAGE:94==
68
CHAPTER 6. THREE TANK CATCHMENT MODEL RESULTS
Lastly, simulations were carried out in order to analyze how the 3TC performance would be affected when
simulating an unknown disturbance for the system, and when the control process is given an estimate of the
disturbance (case 3TCa in table 6.7). It was seen when disturbances are simulated for shorter time intervals
and with larger magnitudes, worse performance was obtained for the system. The results still showed that
the 3TC system achieved promising performance and thus concluded that it was able to handle a disturbance
of that kind.
Note: In general, in order to gain more performance from the WWTP, an option might be considered to
modify the upper boundary limit on tank, T4,V 3, to match the maximum capacity limit of the WWTP52.
Another approach could be implementing a constraint on the weir that divides the wastewater ο§²ow to the sea
and the WWTP. The constraints on the weir can thus be implemented as output linear inequality constraint
which is then also taken into account in the objective function.
52Thus, when weighting the water level in tank, T4,V 3, the maximum capacity of the WWTP is taken into account.
==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER 7
Augmenting the Sewer Network Model
7.1
Nine Tank Catchment Model
The 3TC model is now augmented to emulate a large scale sewer network system, resulting in the nine Tank
Catchment (9TC) model. The augmented model will consist of eight virtual tanks, one real tank and will
be referred to as the 9TC model from this moment on. The same methodology and descriptions of sewer
network components, used to model the 3TC system from Section 4.2, are kept in mind when modelling the
9TC system. The system objectives listed for the 3TC model in Section 4.3 remain the same for the 9TC
system 53.
7.1.1
System Description
A schematic of the 9TC model is provided in ο§¹gure 7.1 and the reader is recommended to view it, in order to
understand the chapter context. The same notation used for the 3TC model in Section 4.3.1 shall be used
here.
Firstly, additional changes are made on the 3TC model: Tank, T1,V 1, is now placed at the beginning of the
sewer network and the disturbance, d1, is now the external inο§²ow to the tank. Moreover, the wastewater
treatment plant, WWTP, is removed from the previous structure and the inο§²ows to the existing overο§²ow
device are modiο§¹ed to be the sum of outο§²ows from tanks T4,V 3 and T5,V 4. Notice, there is a constant steady
ο§²ow to tank T5,V 4, denoted as qc,2, and is one cubic meter per second. Furthermore, only one additional
control variable, u4, is added to the 9TC model description and located at the intersection where a weir
overο§²ow device is used54, as can be seen from the system schematic in ο§¹gure 7.1. Notice that by adding the
control variable, u4, the inο§²ows to tanks T6,V 5 and T7,V 6, are both controlled. Also, eight rainfall areas are
included in the 9TC model and placed at the inlet of each virtual tank, acting as an external inο§²ow.
Furthermore, two wastewater treatment plants, WWTP 1 and WWTP 2, are included in the 9TC model and
placed at the sewer network terminals. Outο§²ows from tanks T8,V 7 and T9,V 8, are inο§²ows to a weir overο§²ow
device and directed to wastewater treatment plants WWTP 1 and WWTP 2, respectively. If the wastewater
treatment plantsβÄô upper boundary limits are exceeded, the weir will overο§²ow and direct the wastewater to the
sea.
53The structure of the augmented model can be related to the Barcelona model from MartΟ≠nez [3].
54See overο§²ow weir device, where the sum of outο§²ows from tanks T4,V 3 and T5,V 4 are the inο§²ow to the weir and ο§²ows in
pipes, u4 and qin,6, outο§²ows from the weir, in ο§¹gure 7.1.

7.1.2
Linear Mathematical Model Representation
The recursion of the tanksβÄô geometric linear equations are provided in eq. 7.1. Notice that tank, T1,V 1, has
been placed after the sewer network entrance and thus the geometric linear equations of the 3TC model in
eq. 4.12, differ from corresponding equations of the 9TC model.
T1,V 1(k + 1) = T1,V 1(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (d1(k) βà£qout,1(k))
T2,V 2(k + 1) = T2,V 2(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (u1(k) + d2(k) βà£qout,2(k))
T3,R(k + 1) = T3,R(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (u2(k) βà£u3(k))
T4,V 3(k + 1) = T4,V 3(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (u3(k) + d3(k) + q13(k) + q23(k) βà£qout,4(k))
T5,V 4(k + 1) = T5,V 4(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (d4 βà£qout,5(k))
T6,V 5(k + 1) = T6,V 5(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (u4(k) + d5(k) βà£qout,6(k))
T7,V 6(k + 1) = T7,V 6(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (d6(k) + qin,6(k) βà£qout,7(k))
T8,V 7(k + 1) = T8,V 7(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (d7(k) + qout,6(k) βà£qout,8(k))
T9,V 8(k + 1) = T9,V 8(k) + Ts ¬Ζ (d8(k) + qout,7(k) βà£qout,9(k))
(7.1)
Consequently, eq. 4.13 which was previously used for obtaining the ο§²ow in pipe, q13, from redirection gate,
G1, is altered, and presented in eq. 7.2. Also, notice that the constant inο§²ow, qc, to the 3TC system, is now a
constant inο§²ow to tank, T1,V 1.
Moreover, the weir overο§²ow device consists of both a merging and splitting node, where outο§²ows from
tanks, T4,V 3 and T5,V 4, correspond to the merging node, and pipes, u4 and qin,6, to the splitting node. The
parameter denoted as qin,6, is now the inο§²ow to tank, T7,V 6, which is obtained from the expression presented
in eq. 7.3.
q13(k) = qout,1(k) βà£u1(k)
(7.2)
qin,6(k) = qout,4(k) + qout,5(k) βà£u4(k)
(7.3)
Expressions of the 9TC system state and output equations are presented in eq. 7.4, where the obtained
9TC model matrices Ae, Be, Ex,e and Ce are given in eq. C.1 (Appendix C.1). The system parameters are
denoted as states xe, outputs ye, control inputs ue and disturbance inputs de, as expressed in eq. 7.5.
xe(k + 1) = Ae ¬Ζ xe(k) + Be ¬Ζ ue(k) + Ex,e ¬Ζ de(k)
ye(k) = Ce ¬Ζ xe(k)

7.1.4
Closed-Loop System Implementations
When analyzing the closed-loop system description, only the designed 9TC model will be used. Moreover,
the control strategies considered for the system are the normal and scalar ISOC MPC methods. Same
considerations of the uncertainty in rain forecasting are assumed as before, and thus a control horizon of
2 h (24 samples) is used for the general approach. The 9TC system controllability and observability are
evaluated as before, conο§¹rming that the system is both controllable and observable56.

in Appendix A.
56Shown in Section C.1.2, in Appendix C.

7.1.5
System Constraints
Subsequently, since no additional changes were made to the associated tanks and pipes of the 3TC model,
numerical values for corresponding lower and upper boundary limits are used as before (presented in tables
5.1 through 5.5). Moreover, the numerical values of the boundary limits of the 9TC model (i.e. system
volume levels, outputs, control signals, ο§²ow rates and pipes) are given in tables 7.3 through 7.7, respectively.

7.2
Summary
In this chapter, the 9TC model was introduced and modelled by augmenting the 3TC model. In addition, a
proper description and a schematic of the system, along with modiο§¹cations made on the 3TC model were
provided. Further, the discrete linear equations for the system were introduced along with the systemβÄôs
state space description. Furthermore, numerical values of system parameters58 and constraint limits were
given in tables 7.1 through 7.7 and used for further analysis of the designed 9TC model through closed-loop
simulations.
Additionally, the complete linear inequality constraint equation for control signal u1, of eq. 7.6, was modiο§¹ed
due to the changes made (i.e. as the additional tank, T1,V 1, was placed at the sewer network main inlet).
Lastly, the recursion of the linear inequality constraint equation for control signal u4 were carried out and
the ο§¹nal expression given in eq. 7.8.
58Notice the numerical values presented in table 7.1 and will be used for tanks i.e. volume levels, upper boundary limits and
surface areas, along with the wastewater treatment plantsβÄô capacity limits, are close to ones obtained by MartΟ≠nez [3].
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 8
Results of the Augmented Model
8.1
Introduction
In this chapter, similar tests are performed on the 9TC model, as carried out for the 3TC model. This is done
in order to compare system performance59 and elapsed time of simulations, tCPU, when using the normal and
scalar ISOC MPC methods as a control strategies for the system.
Moreover, analysis are then pursued to evaluate the complexity level involved with each approach, along
with the effect of choosing larger or smaller control horizons for the controller.
The results of the 9TC system will then be compared to the results of the 3TC system in order to evaluate the
effect of controlling a larger sewer network.
8.2
Time Comparison
Now, closed-loop simulations are performed on the designed 9TC model, using the cases from Section 6.2.
The weighting matrices60 used to weigh the system performance index, are given in Appendix C. Notice,
the system receives more ο§²ow due to the constant inο§²ow parameter, qc,2. Thus, when cases 1 to 4 from
Section 6.2, are compared to corresponding cases listed here below, the total volume delivered to the system
is changed. Notice, only the changed factors of each case are listed:
Case 1:
With a total inο§²ow of 288,000 m3.
Case 2:
With a total inο§²ow of 561,300 m3.
Case 3:
With a total inο§²ow of 1,440,000 m3.
Case 4:
With a total inο§²ow of 2,607,300 m3.
Notice that disturbances in de are zeros for cases 1 and 3. Thus, only the constant wastewater inο§²ows, qc
and qc,2, are entering the system. Also, for cases 2 and 4, pulse signals are only simulated for disturbance
area d3, by simulating corresponding signals from ο§¹gure 6.1.
59See control objectives for the system performance from Section 1.3.

8.2.1
Normal and Scalar MPC
In this section, results from closed-loop simulations of the designed 9TC model, for the four cases listed in
Section 8.2, are presented. Numerical data from simulations, further used to compare the elapsed time, tCPU,
and system performance, are provided in tables 8.1 and 8.2. In addition, the evolution of the QP process is
evaluated for each approach, as done for the 3TC model.
The elapsed time, tCPU, of simulation for the normal and scalar ISOC MPC approach are listed in table 8.1
for cases 1 and 2. The advantage of using the scalar technique is well noticed, with the difference between
elapsed time of simulation, tCPU, for case 1 is approximately 24% and around 29% for case 2. The numerical
data for the total overο§²ow volume from corresponding simulations, are given in table 8.2 and compared
in order to evaluate the system performance for both methods. A small but negligible overο§²ow is noticed
for case 2, and thus the system performance considered similar for both MPC methods. It is seen that
the absolute difference between the percentages of total inο§²ow for both methods is only 0.08% with more
overο§²ow occurring when the scalar MPC method is used.

both MPC methods, while in case 4, overο§²ow situations are not as frequent for both MPC approaches.
When the elapsed time, tCPU, is compared between the 3TC and 9TC systems, it is noticed that it takes longer
time to simulate the 9TC system. This result is not surprising since the associated matrices for the controller
design are larger, and therefore the optimization process of the QP solver becomes more time consuming.

simulations are shown. The effect of changing the control horizon is clearly noticed as the elapsed time,
tCPU, for control horizon of 6 samples, is reduced signiο§¹cantly for both normal and scalar MPC methods. For
both methods and horizons, it is as well noticed that more difference is in elapsed time, tCPU, is obtained in
case 2, when a disturbance is entering the system.

capacity. The controller responds to the disturbance by increasing the inο§²ow, u2, to the real tank, T3,R, while
the outο§²ow, u3, from the real tank is decreased, as further observed in ο§¹gure 8.3. Therefore, the volume
level in the real tank starts to rise, as seen in ο§¹gure 8.2a. Notice as well, that the volume level in tank, T4,V 3,
starts to decrease around minute 180, and control signal, u4, to increase, thus increasing wastewater ο§²ow
directed towards treatment plant WWTP 1.

8.4. UNCERTAINTY ANALYSIS OF DISTURBANCE
81
treated, as seen from the numerical data presented in tables 8.6 and 8.7. Thus, the system performance is
considered sufο§¹cient, where the result is based on a known disturbance.
Further, for the heavy rain episode, it is clear from ο§¹gure 8.2d, that the weir overο§²ow device at the second
terminal of the sewer network, is overο§²owing and wastewater is contaminating the sea. This is considered a
small quantity (since the volume of wastewater is only about 2.5% of the total inο§²ow) and compared to the
treated water (which is approximately 97%), the same can be concluded for the system performance during
heavy rain.
For the violent rain episode, an even larger volume change is noticed for the quantity of wastewater that
ο§²ows to sea (only around 8% of the total inο§²ow). Notice, if the difference in total volume directed to sea are
compared between simulations of heavy and violent rain episodes, it is approximately 5.5%, since there is a
large difference between corresponding magnitudes of disturbances rain consequently resulting in a large
difference in total inο§²ows, as seen in table 8.5. When taking these factors into consideration, the increase in
contaminated water ο§²owing to the sea, is not considered serious since the quantity of treated wastewater is
around 92%, as seen in table 8.7. The system performance is thus considered sufο§¹cient64.

Table 8.8: Elapsed time, tCPU, of simulations, moderate, heavy and violent rainfall episodes.
Subsequently, the distribution of wastewater directed to the two treatment plants, WWTP 1 and WWTP 2,
is shown in table 8.6. The wastewater ο§²owing to the two treatment plants seems to be distributed equally
between them. Notice that WWTP 2 receives more wastewater than WWTP 1, as its capacity of treating
contaminated water is larger. This behavior is produced by the weighting chosen for the system and can be
adjusted further.

not much difference between them although the behavior of system responses are changing a lot between
simulations.
The system performance is considered sufο§¹cient in the three simulations, given the circumstances simulated.
As a overall conclusion for the three simulations, no abnormal behavior was observed from the closed-loop
system responses from simulations. The tanks signals responded to the external inο§²ows, de, then as rainfall
episodes have ended the tank signals settle slowly to their steady state values. Therefore it is concluded
as done in Section 4.3.4 and based on evaluations of the simulations carried out in this section that the
closed-loop 9TC system is asymptotically stable.
8.4
Uncertainty Analysis of Disturbance
Now, simulation analyses are performed on the 9TC system, similar to the 3TC system from Section 6.3.3,
and the control performance analyzed for disturbance uncertainties.
For simulations, actual and estimated disturbances entering the system are simulated as pulse signals,
and the 9TC system evaluated for the simulation cases described in table 8.9. System responses of tank
64Notice that differences do not add up in table 8.7, where the volume difference of parameters WWTP total, and Sea total and
the total inο§²ow to the system is not 100%. This is due to the volume levels of the tanksβÄô that have not reached their steady state
value, as the disturbance that entered the system has not been distributed fully throughout the system.

8.5
Summary
In this chapter, several closed-loop simulations were performed on the designed 9TC model, in order to
analyze and compare the system performance for two control strategies i.e. the normal and scalar MPC
methods. The same speciο§¹cations for analysis were used when comparing MPC methods, as used in Section
6.2. The obtained results were compared to the 3TC model in order to evaluate the effect of adding tanks to
the system structure, seeing how it would inο§²uence the performance of the system and computation time.
Consequently, it was concluded in Section 8.2.1, that the optimization process for each sample proved to be
more time consuming for the 9TC system than the 3TC system.
The results regarding system performance and computation time were then compared to the 3TC system and
showed that they were similar. The trade-off between faster computation time and the system performance is
noticed for the 9TC system as well. Thus, when longer control horizons are chosen, the simulation process
==PAGE:110==
84
CHAPTER 8. RESULTS OF THE AUGMENTED MODEL
becomes more time consuming, but with more stability ensured by the controller and a more sufο§¹cient
performance obtained for the system.
Additionally, simulations were performed to evaluate the system responses and performance, when solely
using the normal MPC method as control strategy. Moderate, heavy and violent rainfall episodes were
simulated for certain rain areas and time intervals. Thus, three simulations where performed and the effect
on system performance for different disturbance magnitudes compared. Further, it was concluded that the
system performance, given the circumstances simulated, were sufο§¹cient for the moderate, heavy and violent
rainfall episodes.
Lastly, simulations were performed to analyze the effect of simulating disturbances by including uncertainty
in rain predictions. The results were similar compared to ones obtained for the 3TC system. It was thus
concluded that the 9TC system is also able to handle uncertainties of this kind.
==PAGE:111==
CHAPTER 9
Conclusion and Future Work
9.1
Conclusion
In this thesis, a simple modelling method has been proposed in order to model and control a sewer network,
to verify its applicability for advanced control strategy and to analyze which factors were affecting computa-
tional effort.
Firstly, by studying different methods for modelling sewer networks based on simpliο§¹cations, the virtual
tank (VT) method was chosen for this thesis. The control method decided upon was the MPC approach,
chosen for its unique ability to include system boundary conditions in the optimization process.
Speciο§¹cations of components associated with the VT modelling method were presented and used to model
the 3TC and 9TC sewer network models. Then, the closed-loop system descriptions of the designed models
were analyzed and the normal ISOC MPC method implemented. The ISOC MPC approach was conο§¹rmed
to be correctly implemented when compared with the ο§¹nite LQR. Then, by performing system response
analyses for both designed models in closed-loop, it was established that both systems were stable.
In order to simplify the control complexity further, a scalar ISOC MPC approach was implemented and
compared to the normal ISOC MPC method, with a control horizon of Nc = 24 samples. Analysis of
elapsed simulation time, tCPU, showed differences of approximately 30% between the normal and scalar
MPC methods, for both the 3TC and 9TC system. It was seen that less computational effort was required by
the scalar ISOC MPC approach and that the quantity of overο§²ow volume from simulations were similar for
both MPC methods and systems.
The elapsed time of the QP solver, tQP, were analyzed and it was seen that, tQP, was much shorter than the
system sampling time, Ts, for both systems and MPC methods. This showed that the time it takes the QP
solver to obtain a solution to the QP problem, should not be problematic and thus the solution not obsolete
when delivered to the system.
The 3TC and 9TC systems were then analyzed by using different control horizons for the normal and scalar
ISOC MPC methods, resulting in a trade-off between computation time and system performance, where
a shorter control horizon yielded faster computation time, but at the cost of causing more overο§²ow in the
system.
The thesis results are promising given the circumstances simulated for systems.
Further, the methodology of MPC in the case of controlling a sewer network, showed sufο§¹cient results and
can be used for ongoing study of sewer networks.
85
==PAGE:112==
86
CHAPTER 9. CONCLUSION AND FUTURE WORK
9.2
Future Work
As the approach carried out in this thesis is only based on a model, further work should be considered in
order to achieve more realistic results.
Firstly, the linear system could be obtained by applying the Saint Venant partial differential equations to a
nonlinear system.
Secondly, the rainfall area absorption factor should be included in order to emulate the rain effect more
realistically during simulation. With the use of real data when simulating rain events, uncertainty analyses
can be performed with better precision, making it easier to evaluate system quality. Also using real data for
rainfall magnitudes, would provide more realistic system performance.
Furthermore, tuning procedures for the system controller could be pursued, in order to achieve desired
performance.
Lastly, including wastewater transportation delays of pipes, could be implemented for better accuracy.

APPENDIX A
Rain Intensity
The main factors inο§²uencing sewer networks are rainfalls and their intensities. Notice that real rain data
measurements are not used when simulating rainfalls in this thesis, but in order to have some knowledge of
rainfall magnitudes (disturbances d and de), thus rain intensities, It, for real events are evaluated. The rain
intensities are categorized as light, moderate, heavy and violent [22], as listed below:
βÄΔ Light rain, when the precipitation rate is < 2.5 mm/h.
βÄΔ Moderate rain, when the precipitation rate is between 2.5 to 10 mm/h
βÄΔ Heavy rain, when the precipitation rate is between 10 and 50 mm/h
βÄΔ Violent rain, when the precipitation rate is > 50 mm/h
The rain intensities used refers to short periods of rainfall and for long time periods these intensities
considered very extreme.

APPENDIX B. 3TC ENHANCED MATERIAL
The system responses of the tanks volume levels from simulations, are shown in ο§¹gure B.1. The disturbance
d2, is an external inο§²ow to tank T2,V 2, and the system settling times will thus be based on associated signals
in tank T2,V 2. Moreover, it is also noticed that disturbance d2, is effecting the volume levels in tanks T3,R
and T4,V 3, as is expected. Since all wastewater that enters the system eventually ο§²ows through tank T4,V 3,
and also since control effort is applied during simulations. Thus, the system compensates for the disturbance,
and tries to maintain the steady state volume levels in all tanks, and the effect depended on the weighting
chosen for the 3TC system67.
Further, the settling times are similar for tank, T2,V 2, from simulations, and approximately 150 min in all
simulations. The slower responses are noticed as well for signals in tanks, T3,R and T4,V 3, as is expected.
The settling time (βâà150 min) is considered to be within reasonable limits or around 30 sample periods, as
the sampling time is 5 min.

solution from the QP solver, which means that the performance demanded of the system, becomes too much
(also considering the simulated circumstances).

B.10
Uncertainty Analyses of Disturbances
Rainfall estimations are complex processes when used for real applications, and the quality of estimations
depends on the uncertainty of the rain forecast. Approaches are thus conducted in this section in order to
analyze errors in estimations by simulating different types of unknown disturbances. Moreover, simulations
of the system for known and unknown disturbances, are carried out and their responses compared and
evaluated.

B.10.2
Random Disturbance
The violent rain episode simulated in Section 6.3.1 are now used again, where a random value is added to the
disturbances, d2 and d3, entering the actual system for each sample. Notice, since disturbance, d1, is not an
external inο§²ow to a tank, the uncertainty of rain forecasting is not considered73. Also, there is no additional
change made on the disturbance entering the estimated system74. Moreover, the uncertainties are only added
to disturbance, d, magnitudes greater than zero. The sensors are assumed to indicate that it is not raining for
disturbances d = 0, as seen in ο§¹gure B.10.
Furthermore, the estimated rain disturbance simulated in ο§¹gure B.10, for magnitudes greater than zero are
normally distributed and varies within the interval ¬±1 m3/s for d2, and ¬±2 m3/s for d3. Notice that the
uncertainty intervals are not chosen randomly, as the total volume to the system is comparable to the total
volume from simulation carried out in Section 6.3.1, for system performance comparison.

B.11
Real Tank Types of Constraints
Simulations are performed to evaluate whether it would be beneο§¹cial to have hard constraints on the real
tank, T3,R. Simulations are conducted by using speciο§¹cations of simulations carried out in Section 6.3,
where effects of simulating average and violent rainstorms where analyzed. One additional simulation will
be performed as well, where even more violent rainstorms are simulated.

hard and soft, are compared in table B.15. The elapsed time, tCPU, of simulation from table B.15, shows a
really small difference between having hard or soft constraints on the real tank, for both average and violent
rainstorms.
The difference in overο§²ow, treated wastewater and directed to sea, between having hard or soft constraints
on the real tank, are the same and thus not showed here.

B.12
Simulations when Sampling Time was Changed
As mentioned, sampling time, Ts, used for the time between measurements in a sewer network is usually
large (approximately 1 to 5min). Moreover, since a sampling time of Ts = 300 s is used in this thesis for the
3TC system, it was decided to evaluate the inο§²uence of using a different sampling times, and the system thus
evaluated for sampling times of 60 and 150s.
For the ο§¹rst analysis, the speciο§¹cations for the simulations performed are given in Section 6.2.1, where case
1 is used. Another simulation is performed as well, where speciο§¹cations from Section 6.3.1 are used for the
two different sampling times.
Firstly, the elapsed time, tCPU, of simulations is evaluated from table B.17, and it is seen that tCPU, becomes
much longer, as shorter sampling times are used for simulation. This behavior is not surprising as the
control horizon has not been changed and the sizes of the MPC matrices thus become larger as shorter
sampling times are used. This will thus affect the time it takes the QP solver to ο§¹nd a solution, require more
computation effort.

tQP, is much smaller than corresponding sampling times in both cases and thus not an issue for the QP solver.
From this it is noticed that choosing smaller sampling times will give larger computation time, tCPU, due to
the larger matrices obtained for the QP solver, when the control horizon is kept constant.

APPENDIX C. 9TC ENHANCED MATERIAL
C.1.1
Stability Analysis
The system stability is evaluated as before by performing robustness analysis for the closed-loop system
using the designed model. As the open-loop system is marginally stable due to the real tank eigenvalue,
placed on the unit circle and since stability is not guaranteed when using MPC as a control strategy. The
controllability and observability matrices for the 9TC system are obtained using eq. 4.20. The controllability
matrix, Mc, is thus computed as a (9Ο½108) dimensional matrix, with rank 9. Therefore, as the number of
rows is equal to its rank, the 9TC system is reachable and thus controllable.
The observability matrix, Mo, is then computed as a (81Ο½9) dimensional matrix, with rank 9. Therefore, as
the number of columns is equal to its rank, the 9TC system is observable as well.
C.1.2
Tuning Weighting Matrices
The tuning of weighting matrices for outputs, inputs and slack variables are performed by using the approach
conducted in Section 5.2. In Section 8.2, the same conο§¹gurations used in Section 6.2, are used for comparison
of closed-loop simulations when using the normal and scalar MPC methods. Thus, the two control methods
and the two systems (i.e. the 3TC and 9TC models), can be compared. Thus the same weights are used for
corresponding tanks of the 3TC system. The weighting matrices used for closed simulations in Section 8.2,
for outputs, inputs and slack variables, Qw, Rw, P and p, are then presented in eqs. C.2 and C.3 respectively.

treatment plants, as circumstances for given time instance in the system, reο§²ects the performance of the two

C.1.5
The Nonlinear and Linear Time Varying Systems
The 9TC nonlinear model is obtained by using same approach used for the 3TC model from Section 4.4.
The tanks volumes, V, of the system is now redeο§¹ned as shown in eq. C.4, while variable œï, from eq.
4.24 remains unchanged. The Linear Time Varying (LTV) system matrix, Ae,t, of the 9TC system is then
presented in eq. C.5. A closed-loop simulation is performed to evaluate how well the LTV system is able to
follow the nonlinear system.
The tanks volume, V, of the 9TC system, becomes:

performance of the known. This result is suspected do to the fact that the known has the correct knowledge
about what is entering the system, since the performance is similar therefore it is concluded that the system
can handle this kind of uncertainty.

APPENDIX D
Simulations with Time Delays
Different types of delays are added to the 3TC model in this appendix, in order to analyze the inο§²uence
of adding a delay to the system. Further, time delays can be added to the system in order to include the
transportation times of the ο§²ow in pipes for instance, as it will take the sewage water sometime to travel
from one point to another.
Three delay types will be considered: input delay, output delay, and input and output delay, respectively.
For each approach, the delay applied on the system is of one sample (Ts = 300s). The effects of these time
delays are then evaluated, when a step is made on disturbance d2, on the closed-loop system when normal
ISOC MPC is used as control strategy. For all approaches, the disturbance signals simulated can be seen in
ο§¹gure D.1, with a simulation duration of 10 hours. Notice, the general 3TC system is simulated as well, for
comparison.

APPENDIX D. SIMULATIONS WITH TIME DELAYS
D.3.1
Summary of Simulations with Delays
Several closed-loop simulations were performed in order to evaluate the effect of adding delays to the 3TC
system when using ISOC MPC as control strategy.
It was noticed that by adding a time delay to the input, output and input-output delay, did not affect the
system very much, with similar behavior in system responses compared to the system without any delay,
for all tests. Since we are not comparing our model with real data it is decided to not include time delays
to the system. If we would be comparing our system to a real one, some time delays would be needed to
compensate for pipe transportation time to achieve more accurate results.
==PAGE:159==
APPENDIX E
LQ Tuning Method with Receding Horizon
E.1
Comparison Between the LQ Method and Unconstrained MPC
In this section, another control method is introduced: the LQ tuning method with receding horizon, referred
to as the LQ method. The approach is to solve the Riccati equation (similar as in Section 5.4 ) for the control
horizon, Nc, for each step of the simulation, where system constraints are evaluated as well, to ensure they
will not be violated. Moreover, to handle the constraints and to achieve the desired performance for the
system, weighting matrices, Qw and Rw, are changed for the intervals where constraints are being violated.
This is repeated until a solution is found, or if maximum number of iterations are exceeded (a maximum
iteration value that is chosen), and the program moves to the next time step.
A simulation is carried out with a duration of 10 hours and disturbance d1, is stepped on from k = 50 and
throughout the simulation, as can be seen from ο§¹gure E.1.
The weighting matrices, Qw and Rw, in eq. E.1, were used as a starting point.

In ο§¹gure E.2, the LQ method is compared to the unconstrained MPC to ensure that implementations are
correct, and to evaluate the computational effort, tCPU. When ο§¹gures E.2a and E.2b are evaluated, it is noticed

implementations are correct.
In ο§¹gure E.2c, it is seen that pipe signal q13, is violating its upper boundary limit. In order to achieve a
better outcome from simulation, several tuning approaches can be pursued: the weight for control signal u1,
the set-point weight on the virtual tank, T2,V2, or the weight on the set-point in tank T4,V3 (due to system
structure).

E.2
Tuning Weighting for Constraints Handling
The simulation routine from Section E.1 is repeated and since pipe q13 is violating its boundary, the lower
boundary constraint for control signal u1, is activated. It was chosen to change the weight on the control
signal u1, and programmed to increase the weighting when lower boundaries are met and repeated until
boundaries are fulο§¹lled (or until maximum iterations are met).
